    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer10412828247736533461">It is 1967. In separate wings of a Viennese hospital, two men lie bedridden. The narrator, Thomas Bernhard, is stricken with a lung ailment; his friend Paul, nephew of the celebrated philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein, is suffering from one of his periodic bouts of madness. As their once-casual friendship quickens, these two eccentric men begin to discover in each other a pos</span>
  <span id="freeText10412828247736533461" style="display:none">It is 1967. In separate wings of a Viennese hospital, two men lie bedridden. The narrator, Thomas Bernhard, is stricken with a lung ailment; his friend Paul, nephew of the celebrated philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein, is suffering from one of his periodic bouts of madness. As their once-casual friendship quickens, these two eccentric men begin to discover in each other a possible antidote to their feelings of hopelessness and mortality — a spiritual symmetry forged by their shared passion for music, a strange sense of humor, disgust for bourgeois Vienna, and fear in the face of death. Part memoir, part fiction, <i>Wittgenstein’s Nephew</i> is both a meditation on the artist’s struggle to maintain a solid foothold in a world gone incomprehensibly askew, and an eulogy to a real-life friendship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10412828247736533461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2446208946" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2446208946" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2446208946?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=german-language">german-language</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=biography-biographical">biography-biographical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=age-ageing">age-ageing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2446208946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12212247402491518586"><b>A Philosophy Of and For the Curmudgeon</b><br/><br/>A catalogue of pet-hates and prejudices masquerading as a memoir, <i>Wittgenstein’s Nephew</i> is a perennial rant of the old against not just the young but against the world in general. This is a world of unmet expectations, incivility, and bad taste. The only possible response to this world is a resigned snobbishness accompanied by the occasional whine of despair. Paul, the nephew of the philosopher, is simply a foil for presenting this as a philosophy rather tha</span>
  <span id="freeText12212247402491518586" style="display:none"><b>A Philosophy Of and For the Curmudgeon</b><br /><br />A catalogue of pet-hates and prejudices masquerading as a memoir, <i>Wittgenstein’s Nephew</i> is a perennial rant of the old against not just the young but against the world in general. This is a world of unmet expectations, incivility, and bad taste. The only possible response to this world is a resigned snobbishness accompanied by the occasional whine of despair. Paul, the nephew of the philosopher, is simply a foil for presenting this as a philosophy rather than as merely an experience.<br /><br />Paul and the author share much the same view. For example, Austrians are perfidious toads who appreciate nether art nor culture. Hospitals and the medical establishment exist to torture their patients using <i>“the most inhuman, murderous, and deadly methods... Of all medical practitioners, psychiatrists [a Viennese creation] are the most incompetent, having a closer affinity to the sex killer than to their science.” </i>The sick are in any case insufferable - when they are sick because they are inhuman and when they become well because they believe they should have human rights once again. <br /><br />The world of course is in a constant state of deterioration for the old: <i>“where the food has always been cheap but was still of excellent quality, as it no longer is.”</i> Because the old see the world in the round, as it were, they have little trust in it, <i>“being unable to contemplate the beauties of nature without at the same time contemplating its malignity and implacability, I fear it and avoid it whenever I can.”</i> Only the old can appreciate the aversion to life produced by experience.<br /><br />A certain cynicism and irrational obstinacy is therefore inevitable. Public recognition is simply receiving pearls from the swine to whom one has given them in the past: <i>“For a prize is always awarded by incompetents who want to piss on the recipient.”</i> The only thing that makes such recognition tolerable is the cash that comes with it, if it comes at all. But eventually even the cash doesn’t compensate for the debasement one suffers at the hands of the ignorant. Is it any wonder, therefore that one eventually becomes obsessed with trivial details: <i>“I also realized at the time that no one with intellectual pretensions could possibly exist in a place where the <i>...</i>Neue Zürcher Zeitung is unobtainable.”</i> Survival consists of maintaining routine after all.<br /><br />At bottom of course is not so much the certainty of impending death but the terrible, uncertain threat of living madness, largely self-induced: <i>“I had behaved toward myself and everything else with the same unnatural ruthlessness that one day destroyed Paul and will one day destroy me. For just as Paul came to grief through his unhealthy overestimation of himself and the world, I too shall sooner or later come to grief through my own unhealthy overestimation of myself and the world.”</i> The madness to be feared is precisely oneself.<br /><br />There is something peculiarly Viennese about this impending madness for Bernhard and for Paul Wittgenstein. And it seems to affect intellectuals uniformly, <i>“In the same way Nietzsche’s mind exploded, just as all the other mad philosophical minds exploded, because they could no longer sustain the pace. Their intellectual fortune builds up at a faster and fiercer rate than they can discard it, then one day the mind explodes and they are dead.”</i> Or perhaps this is merely an example of languid Viennese sarcasm. The import is the same: one’s intellectual capacity simply and inevitably evaporates - a mental sterilisation far worse than the accompanying sexual decline.<br /><br />In <i>Wittgenstein’s Nephew</i>, Bernhard shows himself a master of self-mockery: an entertaining tale of the old at their best and their worst. My wife thinks I should study it carefully.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12212247402491518586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_156502201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156502201" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156502201?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400077564" href="/book/show/6327556-wittgenstein-s-nephew">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=20-ce">20-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=austria">austria</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=translation">translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156502201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9474507261345219541">A highly readable work of dazzling intensity. The novella is based in part on a true story: author Bernhard's friendship with philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein's grand nephew, Paul. Prepare yourself for a blast of intellectually dense but very compelling--and funny--writing. The book is at bottom a great howl of rage against death. Bernhard in his day (1931-1989) was perhaps Austria's most controversial novelist/playwright. The narrator, based on Bernhard, and his familiar, Paul Wittgenstein, shar</span>
  <span id="freeText9474507261345219541" style="display:none">A highly readable work of dazzling intensity. The novella is based in part on a true story: author Bernhard's friendship with philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein's grand nephew, Paul. Prepare yourself for a blast of intellectually dense but very compelling--and funny--writing. The book is at bottom a great howl of rage against death. Bernhard in his day (1931-1989) was perhaps Austria's most controversial novelist/playwright. The narrator, based on Bernhard, and his familiar, Paul Wittgenstein, share a rare friendship. They're the sort of people who laugh at others in public places out of a false sense of superiority. They are compassion-free. Neither ever transcends his own boyish rage. Bernhard, in fact, made his name on his rage. Paul Wittgenstein's rage, by contrast, turned to madness. <br /><br />We start with the Bernhard character lying in the lung ward of a Vienna hospital, the Wilhelminenberg. Here he begins the tale of his friendship with Paul. Bernhard is in the lung ward, and Paul is in the mental health ward. They have astonishingly similar tastes. They both love philosophy and music. Paul, like his famous relation, is from a family of mercantilists (munitions, I think). And Bernhard does his best to paint them as philistines, notoriously hostile to art and culture. Paul, like Ludwig, must reject his family if he is to survive. For the first half of his life, he is fabulously rich and travels widely. Then he exhausts his pile and must live like a pauper for the rest of his days. He's in and out of the Wilhelminenberg mental facility every six months. There he receives shock treatments and is locked into a cage that surrounds his bed. Bernhard describes Paul's treatments as a kind of breaking of his spirit. Once his spirit is broken and his weight dramatically down he is released. Then the cycle starts over again. <br /><br />But wait--it occurs to me now that I was not really precise in saying that the two friends are compassion-free. Certainly, based on this brief text, it can be said that the two main characters' friendship, no doubt intellectually rich, was floated upon a certain cynical rage and hatred of others. <i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i> by contrast is an exercise--albeit a tardy one--in compassion. Bernhard has written this tribute to his friend in which he excoriates himself for abandoning Paul during his final sad days. But he could not, being an invalid himself, meet death face on. He was too afraid. He admits his cowardice. So <i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i> is Bernhard's apologia. He wants us to know who his friend was and how he failed him. He is nothing if not painfully honest. A wrenching but enthralling novella.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9474507261345219541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2528865309" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2528865309" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2528865309?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400077564" href="/book/show/6327556-wittgenstein-s-nephew">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=austria">austria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2528865309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3505844289848125044">Damn, I was hoping to drag this out for as long as possible, but how does one drag out a book with only a hundred pages and no paragraphs?. Added to the fact it was just so darn good. It would have been like trying to stop an Olympic Skier flying down a mountain. So Stuff it, I thought, I will rip through this in no time, stop for a coffee, and read it again. Before deciding which of his books I will order next whilst scurrying around in a cold sweat. Yes, it's safe to say Mr. Bernhard has left </span>
  <span id="freeText3505844289848125044" style="display:none">Damn, I was hoping to drag this out for as long as possible, but how does one drag out a book with only a hundred pages and no paragraphs?. Added to the fact it was just so darn good. It would have been like trying to stop an Olympic Skier flying down a mountain. So Stuff it, I thought, I will rip through this in no time, stop for a coffee, and read it again. Before deciding which of his books I will order next whilst scurrying around in a cold sweat. Yes, it's safe to say Mr. Bernhard has left a deep impression on me. It was like climbing into his head. And wandering through the hospital ward of his mind. <br /><br />In a nutshell, the short novel, which is very autobiographical in nature - a writer, not unlike Bernhard himself, and the brilliant but troubled....no, not troubled, more like mad nephew of the philosopher Wittgenstein are confined to beds in the same hospital, the narrator in the pulmonary ward and Paul Wittgenstein in the asylum. Both are plagued with terminal thoughts about the fears and doubts associated with the nature of life. Acquaintances beforehand, they reach out to bond and build a friendship based on mutual support and respect that somehow thrives in this bleak and hopeless environment of the sick and the dying.<br /><br />Bernhard's style relies on ponderous repetition of words and ideas, that felt like an off key musical composition with maddeningly convoluted sentences that tend to numb the mind. That's a compliment, not a criticism. Thomas Bernhard was simply a wonderful wordsmith, he weaves his story in riffs, and the most surreal, darkly funny, and daringly provocative of tapestries. The author discourses a lot about health and mortality. He had lived his life near death for a long time and compares death of the body to death of the spirit or mind. He resents healthy people who he feels are hypocrites and truly hate sick people. I get the feeling Bernhard wrote this as a sort of way to seek forgiveness for the fact he abandoned his friend towards the end of his life.<br /><br />With a chronically diseased body, he looked existence in the eye and declared it a farce, and this slender offering is a justification that both he and Paul developed their lives in the face of death and madness, year after year, and are necessarily different from healthy people, who may lack the capacity to share their feelings properly. A compressed work of utter brilliance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3505844289848125044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_742433824" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/742433824" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/742433824?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21248612-wittgenstein-s-nephew">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=bernhard-thomas">bernhard-thomas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer742433824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8077754112127470820"><br/><i> Wittgenstein’s Nephew </i> is a thing about Paul, nephew of a great though rather dismissed in native country philosopher, Ludwig Wittgenstein; is a thing about illness and falling into madness ; at last is a thing about dying and death. But most of all <i> Wittgenstein’s Nephew </i> is a thing about friendship and its nature. <br/><br/>In 1967, at the same time though in two separated wings of hospital, resides our narrator ( due to lungs disease ) whilst his friend Paul Wittgenstein is under psychiatric care. Fro</span>
  <span id="freeText8077754112127470820" style="display:none"><br /><i> Wittgenstein’s Nephew </i> is a thing about Paul, nephew of a great though rather dismissed in native country philosopher, Ludwig Wittgenstein; is a thing about illness and falling into madness ; at last is a thing about dying and death. But most of all <i> Wittgenstein’s Nephew </i> is a thing about friendship and its nature. <br /><br />In 1967, at the same time though in two separated wings of hospital, resides our narrator ( due to lungs disease ) whilst his friend Paul Wittgenstein is under psychiatric care. From Bernhard’s writing slowly unfolds story of their friendship, the first meeting, mutual interests in music and opera, similar sense of humour, the same aversion to the country and love to the city <i> when I am in the country, bereft of mental stimulus, my thinking atrophies, because my whole mind atrophies , but nothing so calamitous ever happens to me in the city </i>, adhering to peculiar custom sitting in famous Viennese cafes and ridiculing behaviours and flaws of other clients since <i> most of the minds we associate with are housed in heads that have little more to offer than overgrown potatoes </i>, sharing some restless soul that forced them to live constantly on the move, toing and froing, still escaping from one place to another.<br /><br />Of course Thomas Bernhard would not have been himself if in this slim novella, in own unique style, dripping with black humour, full of meandering and repetitive phrases, obsessive and spiteful comments, had not taken own compatriots ‘ ignorance and stupidity apart. There is any sacred cow for him, no one is to escape his sarcasm and derision, no matter ministers <i> stupidity was written all over his face, as it is over the faces of all ministers without exception</i> or actors from famous Burgtheater <i> the world’s first theatrical whorehouse </i>, treats them all with equal spitefulness and acerbity.<br /><br />Friendship is a gift, a rare thing that we still have to care and that way novel reads as a praise and hymn to it. Each one of us has own definition of friendship, when we’re saying <i> friend </i> we may see someone we'd like to share secrets, thoughts and interests while for others friend means someone with whom we may remain silent not feeling any awkwardness, any need to talking. But sometimes that bond is too fragile and we ourselves too weak and unreliable to remain faithful to that feeling. And with this writing Bernhard pays delayed homage to his eccentric friend, for own reluctance and cowardice in the hour of trial.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8077754112127470820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2746304470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2746304470?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emilio Berra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emilio Berra" href="/user/show/69897903-emilio-berra">Emilio Berra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788845907265" href="/book/show/9697138-il-nipote-di-wittgenstein">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69897903-emilio-berra?shelf=letto">letto</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2746304470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15667264446968171559"> Storia di un'amicizia<br/><br/>Uno dei libri della piena maturità di T. Bernhard.<br/>E' la storia di un'amicizia maschile tra il narratore e Paul, appunto il nipote del celebre filosofo Wittgenstein. Un testo molto bello che, man mano che procede, coinvolge sempre più il lettore.<br/>La musica appassiona entrambi. Una vena di follia li accomuna.<br/><br/>Paul passò da una ricchezza rilevante a una dignitosa povertà. Della propria famiglia, "aveva sempre detto che era una famiglia nemica dell'arte e dello spirito, e soffoca</span>
  <span id="freeText15667264446968171559" style="display:none"> Storia di un'amicizia<br /><br />Uno dei libri della piena maturità di T. Bernhard.<br />E' la storia di un'amicizia maschile tra il narratore e Paul, appunto il nipote del celebre filosofo Wittgenstein. Un testo molto bello che, man mano che procede, coinvolge sempre più il lettore.<br />La musica appassiona entrambi. Una vena di follia li accomuna.<br /><br />Paul passò da una ricchezza rilevante a una dignitosa povertà. Della propria famiglia, "aveva sempre detto che era una famiglia nemica dell'arte e dello spirito, e soffocata dai suoi stessi milioni". Lui e il famoso zio erano pertanto guardati con imbarazzo.<br />Il narratore prova grande ammirazione per l'amico : "in tutta la mia vita non avevo mai conosciuto un essere umano che possedesse un'acuta capacità di osservazione e una più grande ricchezza intellettuale". "Quando sentivo che in me tutto era morto o quasi, una visita a Paul era sempre bastata per ridare la vita (...) al mio pensiero musicale". "L'unica differenza tra Paul e me è che Paul si è lasciato 'completamente' dominare dalla sua pazzia (...), io non mi sono mai lasciato dominare dalla mia pazzia, peraltro non meno grande della sua".<br /><br />L'io narrante, con sguardo leale e impietoso soprattutto verso se stesso, continua a parlare anche di sé, con franchezza e senza autogiustificazioni ; anzi rappresentandosi con inusuale severità.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15667264446968171559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_320048015" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/320048015" />
    <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez"><img alt="Paquita Maria Sanchez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594814608p2/2508169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/320048015?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paquita Maria Sanchez" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez">Paquita Maria Sanchez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer320048015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12975087372243766708">Fick! What started out as the cause of me once again whining under my breath about Bernhard's head-clubbing repetition slowly evolved into a rewarding, mist upon the eyes causing, scrupulous bloodletting of Bernhard's personal guilt, nostalgia, and self-reflection resulting from the passing of his closest friend, literally Wittgenstein's nephew, Paul Wittgenstein. Paul was, as Bernhard and various Mental Health Professionals proclaimed, a certifiable Madman. However, Paul argued he was at least </span>
  <span id="freeText12975087372243766708" style="display:none">Fick! What started out as the cause of me once again whining under my breath about Bernhard's head-clubbing repetition slowly evolved into a rewarding, mist upon the eyes causing, scrupulous bloodletting of Bernhard's personal guilt, nostalgia, and self-reflection resulting from the passing of his closest friend, literally Wittgenstein's nephew, Paul Wittgenstein. Paul was, as Bernhard and various Mental Health Professionals proclaimed, a certifiable Madman. However, Paul argued he was at least less mad than his famous--yet posthumously disowned by the family--uncle Ludwig, "the maddest member of the family...to be a multimillionaire and a village schoolteacher is a bit perverse, don't you think?" <br /><br />Through examining their years-long friendship, Bernhard engages in ruminations on mortality, the nature of disease/deep disturbance and their effects on otherwise stable family, friend, and romantic relationships (and, thereby, the fleeting nature of all relationships <i>period</i>), Bernhard turns the scope inward and addresses his own failings as a human being and, by extension, as a friend. In a splattered chronology, Bernhard recalls brief moments of tenderness between himself and his, errr, 'eccentric?' friend coupled with bouts of extreme psychosis and deranged, occasionally illegal behavior on the latter's part. As Bernhard found himself retreating from his friend, he was forced to address a number of painful, oft-suppressed questions as to why he did so. As the novel unfolds, it digs deeper and deeper into the author's demons, these being collective demons. Sweet surface aside, Bernhard slowly flows out the truth of why he inevitably let Paul go, and in doing so, who He is and We are. To be brief, this is a therapy session on paper, but constructed with so much restraint from glamorizing or self-aggrandizing, in fact so intent on self-lacerating, that it becomes not only a moving, selfless memoir of a tragic friend met with a tragic destiny, but also a sage meditation on solipsism, artistic ego, philosophical shifts over a lifetime and the contradictions contained therein, and the generally fleeting nature of intimacy and companionship. Despite the previously mentioned repetition at the start of the novel (used as a means to draw comparisons between Bernhard's physical health problems and Paul's mental health problems in flashing neon 'Let me explain like you are a child' fashion), the novel is, on the whole, exquisitely composed. Some passages just slew me. <br /><br /><i>He was a restless character who always lived on his nerves and was perpetually out of control. He was a brooder, endlessly philosophizing and endlessly accusing. He was also an incredibly well trained observer, and over the years he developed his gift for observation to a fine art. He was the most ruthless observer and constantly found occasion to accuse. Nothing escaped his accusing tongue. Those who came under his scrutiny survived only a very short time before being savaged; once they had drawn </i>suspicion<i> upon themselves and become guilty of some </i>crime<i> or at least some </i>misdemeanor<i>, he would lambaste them them with the same words that I myself employ when I am roused to indignation, when I am forced to defend myself and take action against the insolence of the world in order to not to be put down and annihilated by it.</i><br /><br />Hey I know that guy! (Me!)<br /><br />For someone cast off out of combined selfishness and death-fear, Paul Wittgenstein clearly branded Bernhard's hard heart:<br /><br /><i>...before I met my friend there had been a period when I was prey to a morbid melancholy, if not depression, when I really believed I was lost, when for years I did no proper work but spent most of the day in a state of total apathy and often came close to putting an end to my life by my own hand. For years I had taken refuge in a terrible suicide brooding, which deadened my mind and made everything unendurable, above all myself--brooding on the utter futility all around me, into which I had been plunged by my general weakness, but above all my weakness of character. For a long time I could not imagine being </i>able<i> to go on living, or even existing. I was no longer capable of seizing upon any purpose in life that would have given me control over myself. Every morning upon waking I was inevitably caught up in this mechanism of suicide brooding, and I remained in this grip throughout the day. And I was deserted by everyone because </i>I<i> had deserted everyone--that is the truth--because I no longer wanted anyone...It was probably at the height of my despair--a word that I am not ashamed to use, as I no longer intend to deceive myself or gloss over in a society and a world that perpetually seeks to gloss over everything in a most sickening manner--that Paul appeared on the scene...</i><br /><br />Oooof. If that doesn't hit you in your soft, mushy parts, I don't know what to do for you. Have you ever had a friend or family member who was chronically sick and/or dying in one extreme or another, whether emotionally (through mental problems, drug/drink) or inescapably physically (disease, etc)? Do you have any guilt feelings associated with what is happening to or ended up happening to them? Do you wonder why you treat them as well or as poorly, why you hide from or embrace them during their times of distress? Read this. You won't like it, because it's really good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12975087372243766708" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2089306422" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2089306422" />
    <a title="zumurruddu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9637336-zumurruddu"><img alt="zumurruddu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600792365p2/9637336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2089306422?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="zumurruddu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="zumurruddu" href="/user/show/9637336-zumurruddu">zumurruddu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788845907265" href="/book/show/9697138-il-nipote-di-wittgenstein">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9637336-zumurruddu?shelf=mitteleuropa">mitteleuropa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2089306422" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8094870986971422694"><b>“Già non sopporto me stesso, figuriamoci un’orda di persone come me che passano il loro tempo a spremersi il cervello e a scrivere”</b><br/><br/><br/>Quello che mi piace in Bernhard, quello che mi <i>fa bene</i> quando lo leggo è che le sue parole, ogni sua singola parola, e ogni sua ripetizione di quella parola (che non è mai e poi mai di troppo!), dicevo, tutte le sue parole e suoi pensieri sono un sacrosanto e disperato attacco frontale contro l’ipocrisia, tutti i tipi di ipocrisia, un attacco portato, <i>come è naturale</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8094870986971422694" style="display:none"><b>“Già non sopporto me stesso, figuriamoci un’orda di persone come me che passano il loro tempo a spremersi il cervello e a scrivere”</b><br /><br /><br />Quello che mi piace in Bernhard, quello che mi <i>fa bene</i> quando lo leggo è che le sue parole, ogni sua singola parola, e ogni sua ripetizione di quella parola (che non è mai e poi mai di troppo!), dicevo, tutte le sue parole e suoi pensieri sono un sacrosanto e disperato attacco frontale contro l’ipocrisia, tutti i tipi di ipocrisia, un attacco portato, <i>come è naturale</i>, alle sue estreme conseguenze.<br />I suoi risultati migliori li ottiene, a mio parere, quando le sue invettive diventano più che mai estreme e iperboliche, quando la sua arte dell’esagerazione riesce a strappare un riso assurdo, quando riesce a ottenere quello che si può chiamare soltanto “umorismo bernhardiano” perché non vi è nessun’altra definizione disponibile, perché nessuno mai avrebbe pensato di ridere quando tutto viene dipinto come micidiale, ripugnante, vergognoso e deprimente.<br />Queste invettive hanno su di me un potere pacificante e liberatorio, nonostante l’esagerazione, anzi <i>proprio per la loro manifesta esagerazione</i>, proprio perché so che non sono da <i>prendere alla lettera</i>. Le ripetizioni ossessive e martellanti contengono l’esatta dose di rabbia e livore necessari per esorcizzare quelli che covano dentro di me. Ecco un esempio, scusate la lunghezza dell’estratto:<br /><br />“Questa cosiddetta malattia mentale, che non è mai stata classificata con esattezza, era presente in Paul sin dall’infanzia. Già il neonato Paul era stato partorito come un malato mentale, con quella cosiddetta malattia mentale che lo ha poi dominato vita natural durante. Con questa sua cosiddetta malattia mentale Paul è vissuto fino alla morte con la massima naturalezza, così come gli altri vivono <i>senza</i> una simile malattia mentale. In relazione a questa sua cosiddetta malattia mentale, ci sono state testimonianze quanto mai deprimenti della sprovvedutezza dei medici e della scienza medica nel suo insieme. Questa sprovvedutezza dei medici e della loro scienza ha dato di questa cosiddetta malattia mentale di Paul le più diverse e allarmanti definizioni, ma mai quella giusta, naturalmente, perché nella sua dissennatezza la scienza medica non è stata in grado di farlo, e tutte le definizioni che di continuo la scienza medica ha dato riguardo a questa malattia mentale del mio amico si sono rivelate sbagliate o addirittura assurde, e di continuo una definizione smentiva l’altra nella maniera più vergognosa e al tempo stesso più deprimente. I cosiddetti psichiatri definivano la malattia del mio amico ora in un modo ora in un altro, senza mai avere il coraggio di ammettere che per questa, come per tutte le altre malattie, non esiste una definizione giusta, ma sempre e soltanto definizioni sbagliate, sempre e soltanto definizioni fuorvianti, perché gli psichiatri in definitiva, come tutti gli altri medici del resto, usano di continuo le loro definizioni cliniche sbagliate per rendersi più facile la vita, e insomma, da delinquenti quali sono, per dormire tra due guanciali. Ogni momento dicevano la parola maniaco e ogni momento la parola depressivo, e immancabilmente si trattava della parola sbagliata. Ogni momento (come tutti gli altri medici) cercavano riparo in un nuovo termine scientifico per proteggere e tutelare se stessi (ma non certo i pazienti!). Come tutti gli altri medici, anche gli psichiatri che avevano in cura Paul si trinceravano dietro il latino che a poco a poco diventava un baluardo invalicabile e impenetrabile che essi erigevano, come da secoli i loro predecessori, tra sé e i pazienti con l’intento esclusivo di camuffare la propria incompetenza e avvolgere in cortine fumogene la propria cialtroneria. Come muraglia in effetti invisibile ma più che mai impenetrabile, essi mettevano il latino tra sé e le loro vittime già all’inizio del trattamento, i cui metodi, come sappiamo, sono sempre e comunque disumani, criminosi e micidiali. Lo psichiatra è il più incompetente di tutti i medici e in ogni caso è più attirato dallo stupro che dalla scienza. Di niente in vita mia ho avuto più paura che di cadere in mano agli psichiatri, al cui confronto tutti gli altri medici, che pure in ultima analisi altro non portano se non sciagure, sono comunque assai meno pericolosi, perché nella nostra società gli psichiatri si sentono ancora molto solidali tra loro e quindi investiti di una sorta di immunità, e siccome io ho avuto l’opportunità di studiare per moltissimi anni i metodi terapeutici da loro adottati con totale assenza di scrupoli sul mio amico Paul, il timore che già prima nutrivo nei loro confronti è diventato un timore ancora più intenso. Gli psichiatri sono i veri e propri demoni della nostra epoca.”<br /><br /><br />Questo “Nipote di Wittgenstein”, nella sua brevità, l’ho trovata un’opera quanto mai compiuta e riuscita.<br />Sembra incredibile, ma vi assicuro, parla di amicizia. Di come l’amicizia possa arricchire l’esistenza e rendere la vita degna di essere vissuta, anche quando tutto è disperazione e malattia. Ma non aspettatevi retorica e buoni sentimenti: ve l’ho detto, qui per l’ipocrisia non ce n’è.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8094870986971422694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2410896444" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2410896444" />
    <a title="Patrizia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22698711-patrizia"><img alt="Patrizia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503079400p2/22698711.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2410896444?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrizia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrizia" href="/user/show/22698711-patrizia">Patrizia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788845907265" href="/book/show/9697138-il-nipote-di-wittgenstein">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22698711-patrizia?shelf=2-mini-sfida-2019">2-mini-sfida-2019</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2410896444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12933261084439867388">Storia di un’amicizia durata dodici anni, quella tra Paul Wittgenstein e Thomas Bernhard.<br/>Un’amicizia tutt’altro che facile, l’incontro tra due menti geniali, accomunate da tante passioni e divise da altrettanti elementi. Un’amicizia con alti e bassi, fatta di ostinazione, che<br/><br/>“tutto il tempo abbiamo dovuto elaborare con grande fatica sì da poterla conservare nella maniera più utile e vantaggiosa per entrambi, trattandola costantemente con estrema cautela e non dimenticando mai la sua grande fragi</span>
  <span id="freeText12933261084439867388" style="display:none">Storia di un’amicizia durata dodici anni, quella tra Paul Wittgenstein e Thomas Bernhard.<br />Un’amicizia tutt’altro che facile, l’incontro tra due menti geniali, accomunate da tante passioni e divise da altrettanti elementi. Un’amicizia con alti e bassi, fatta di ostinazione, che<br /><br />“tutto il tempo abbiamo dovuto elaborare con grande fatica sì da poterla conservare nella maniera più utile e vantaggiosa per entrambi, trattandola costantemente con estrema cautela e non dimenticando mai la sua grande fragilità”.<br /><br />Con la sua prosa ossessiva, in cui i pensieri si compongono a spirale, ripetuti, analizzati fino allo spasimo, Bernhard ha creato un grande monologo, ripercorrendo i dodici anni di discorsi, serate al caffè, viaggi, osservazioni con Paul Wittgenstein.<br />La malattia nello stesso ospedale, un problema polmonare per lui, la pazzia che richiedeva sempre più frequenti ricoveri di Paul. La morte, che sembrava segnare Paul fin dal primo incontro, quel destino comune a tutti gli uomini, di cui Paul sembrava l’incarnazione vivente.<br />Bernhard rivive il decadimento dell’amico, quel suo invecchiare rapidamente, l’aggravarsi della follia, la solitudine finale.<br /><br />“Negli ultimi mesi della sua vita il signor barone non era ormai che l’ombra di se stesso, come si suol dire, e da quest’ombra, che sempre più assumeva sembianze spettrali, tutti si tenevano sempre più alla larga. E neanch’io, com’è ovvio, avevo con l’ombra di Paul lo stesso rapporto che avevo avuto con Paul in passato. Non ci vedevamo quasi più, noi due, i nostri appuntamenti si erano assai diradati, non foss’altro perché spesso passavano giorni e giorni senza che lui uscisse dal suo appartamento della Stallburggasse”.<br /><br />Il senso di colpa per non avergli più voluto parlare trova giustificazione nella paura della morte<br /><br />“Noi evitiamo gli uomini segnati dalla morte e anch’io ho ceduto a questa infamia. Durante gli ultimi mesi della sua vita, ho consapevolmente evitato il mio amico per ubbidire a un basso istinto di autoconservazione, e questo non me lo perdono”.<br /><br />Diventa allo stesso tempo il senso di colpa del sopravvissuto in una stupenda analisi del suo ritrarsi di fronte alla vista dell’amico ormai incapace di parlare e con la mente ottenebrata da un ultimo fatale attacco di follia<br /><br />“non so se a trattenermi sia stata la mia paura di lui che era ormai diventato la morte stessa, o la mia sensazione di dovergli risparmiare l’incontro con me, io che a quel tratto non ero ancora giunto, probabilmente entrambe le cose”.<br /><br />Paul aveva predetto che<br /><br />“Duecento amici verranno al mio funerale e tu dovrai tenere un discorso sulla mia tomba, mi disse Paul. Ma al suo funerale non vennero più di otto o nove persone, come so, e io stesso in quel momento mi trovavo a Creta”.<br /><br />Dodici anni di amicizia sepolti in un cimitero di Vienna e la dura confessione di Bernhard<br /><br />“Ancora non sono stato a visitare la sua tomba.”</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2826417760898630002">When stress punches you in the stomach with a force that takes your breath (yes, I know, school has just only started for teachers and the kids won't be in until next week, but scheduling is a brutal hellish device that creates an immediate sense of evil foreboding and looming mortality and exhaustion), it is almost impossible to read coherently for pleasure.   <span id="freeText2826417760898630002" style="display:none">When stress punches you in the stomach with a force that takes your breath (yes, I know, school has just only started for teachers and the kids won't be in until next week, but scheduling is a brutal hellish device that creates an immediate sense of evil foreboding and looming mortality and exhaustion), it is almost impossible to read coherently for pleasure. Unfortunately, my main source of detox and soothing is reading, so that is a bit of a Catch-22.<br /><br />But there is one exception to the "too-much-stress-no-literacy" syndrome: I can read Thomas Bernhard's dark reflections any time, and preferably when struck by a severe case of "stress-by-foreboding". There is something incredibly reassuring in opening one of his books and realising that whatever you call this malaise, he has a stronger version of it, and he is able to put it into brilliantly rantish German.<br /><br />To Thomas Bernhard - for he proves that pessimism is relative.</span>

<div id="review_2473178878" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2473178878" />
    <a title="Cosimo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34544240-cosimo"><img alt="Cosimo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1409645199p2/34544240.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2473178878?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cosimo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cosimo" href="/user/show/34544240-cosimo">Cosimo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788845907265" href="/book/show/9697138-il-nipote-di-wittgenstein">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2473178878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17985396553275681661"><b>Come cerchi cosa cerchi</b><br/><br/>“Noi evitiamo gli uomini segnati dalla morte e anche io ho ceduto a questa infamia”.<br/><br/>Il romanzo narra la crescita di una malinconica e passionale amicizia tra due eccentrici e ossessivi personaggi che amano la musica, perseverano in una onestà brutale e sono tormentati da un nauseante disgusto per la borghesia viennese. Sono ricoverati nello stesso ospedale, in due padiglioni diversi, pneumologia e malattia mentale, sanatorio e manicomio. Il testo appare come un diario di r</span>
  <span id="freeText17985396553275681661" style="display:none"><b>Come cerchi cosa cerchi</b><br /><br />“Noi evitiamo gli uomini segnati dalla morte e anche io ho ceduto a questa infamia”.<br /><br />Il romanzo narra la crescita di una malinconica e passionale amicizia tra due eccentrici e ossessivi personaggi che amano la musica, perseverano in una onestà brutale e sono tormentati da un nauseante disgusto per la borghesia viennese. Sono ricoverati nello stesso ospedale, in due padiglioni diversi, pneumologia e malattia mentale, sanatorio e manicomio. Il testo appare come un diario di ricordi intessuto di invenzione: ha forma di confessione, omaggio all'amicizia, discorso funebre, eulogia. I due amici condividono una specie di simmetrica interiorità di fronte alla morte e al dolore. Bernhard sviluppa una prosa nichilista e ansimante che indaga su una realtà oscura, indecifrabile, materia di odio familiare e rancore; scava nei conflitti cresciuti nei nodi irrisolti, esamina follia e onnipotenza sotterranee e vuoti affettivi che minacciano l'integrità di uno spirito circolare e fragile, che fluttua intorno ai complessi, alle disperazioni, ai fallimenti. L'autore sta sempre in cerca di una fortissima tensione che sveli la natura reale e illusoria delle cose. <i>”I morenti ritirano la testa nel guscio e non vogliono avere più niente a che fare con i vivi e con quelli che non pensano alla morte”.</i> I temi che Bernhard affronta colpiscono nel segno: distanza, incomunicabilità, follia e genio, oppressione, egoismo, corruzione e ignavia. La crisi culturale è specchio di un controumanesimo affarista, devoto, solipsista e nazionalista, caratteristica di un mondo che esalta il narcisismo, la vittoria e la vanità, che si identifica in un potere violento e autoritario, incapace di altruismo, dialogo e ascolto, inseguitore di idoli inconsistenti; un potere che segna la malattia con il marchio della vergogna e della colpa. Bernhard rivela con le sue parole profondo rispetto e sincera stima per Paul Wittgenstein, un uomo sensibile e inadatto alla vita, indigente per singolarità, naturale nel vivere come gli altri, come se la malattia non ci fosse, sebbene dominato e sfruttato e reso inerme dalla follia. Insomma il male consuma in modo irriducibile, l'unico rimedio è gettare fuori dalla finestra l'interiorità, l'onta dell’anima, l'impossibile liberazione da sé stessi e dal destino grottesco di ciascuno di noi. Nella follia e nel decadimento, Bernhard riesce a tracciare una filosofia, praticando un'apertura per l'animo che anela a respirare. E suggerisce un'altra cosa rilevante: come nel titolo di quel carteggio, l'amicizia è la vera patria.<br /><br />“Non c’è ipocrisia più diffusa di quella del sano nei confronti del malato. I sani, in fondo, non vogliono avere più niente a che fare con i malati e non sono affatto contenti che i malati, sto parlando dei veri malati, e cioè dei malati gravi, esigano tutt’a un tratto di ritornare in buona salute, o almeno di normalizzarsi o almeno di migliorare le loro condizioni di salute. Il sano, se è una persona sincera, ammetterà che non vuole avere più niente a che fare con il malato, non vuole che nessuno gli rammenti la malattia e, attraverso la malattia, logicamente e forzatamente la morte”.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17985396553275681661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_131619539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131619539" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3073544-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472052100p2/3073544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131619539?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3073544-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400077564" href="/book/show/6327556-wittgenstein-s-nephew">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3073544-david?shelf=thomas-bernhard">thomas-bernhard</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3073544-david?shelf=misery-loves-company">misery-loves-company</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131619539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer578727366366076832">A quickie review, so put on your non-porous splash suit and buckle yourself in. Eschewing his emblematic deranged, run-on style, Bernhard serves up <i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i> as both a eulogy of his friend Paul Wittgenstein, the famed philosopher's mentally unbalanced nephew, and a bleak rumination on death -- or more pointedly, the slow, surreptitious death that constitutes life. If you know someone who is despairing about about his or her physical deterioration and impending death, do not be so tho</span>
  <span id="freeText578727366366076832" style="display:none">A quickie review, so put on your non-porous splash suit and buckle yourself in. Eschewing his emblematic deranged, run-on style, Bernhard serves up <i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i> as both a eulogy of his friend Paul Wittgenstein, the famed philosopher's mentally unbalanced nephew, and a bleak rumination on death -- or more pointedly, the slow, surreptitious death that constitutes life. If you know someone who is despairing about about his or her physical deterioration and impending death, do not be so thoughtful as to give this book to said person as a gift. As the saying goes, 'Misery loves company' but death, as <i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i> makes clear, is a solitary endeavor. It can not be shared. Even simultaneous, proximal deaths are not shared -- just juxtaposed. The narrative of this pithy novel is less a conventional plot point A to plot point B affair than a rueful meandering. The impetus is the narrator's (i.e., Thomas Bernhard's) stay in a lung disease ward in Vienna -- where prevailing medical opinion agrees that Bernhard will die, imminently. Adjacent to the lung disease ward in a spatial relation that is not quite clear to me is the mental ward, where Paul Wittgenstein is temporarily residing. Bernhard describes the medical complex (quite specifically) as <i>only</i> a mental ward and a lung disease ward. Nothing more. Which seems an odd commingling, but nevertheless... it affords him the opportunity (or the hypothetical opportunity) to visit his friend. The meeting doesn't go well, however. Something is lost, something is missing. Everything has changed. Mortality and illness ruin everything. Lock up the handgun and booze because this won't go down easy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="578727366366076832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13374213794462160581"><i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i> is about friendship but in some strange way it reminded me of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/333538.The_Castle' title='The Castle by Franz Kafka' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Castle</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5223.Franz_Kafka' title='Franz Kafka' rel='nofollow noopener'>Franz Kafka</a>…<br/>Between a man and freedom there is always a wall of bureaucracy…<br/>“Paul’s mind quite simply exploded because he could not discard his intellectual fortune fast enough. In the same way Nietzsche’s mind exploded, just as all the other mad philosophical minds exploded, because they could no longer sustain the pace. Their intellectual fortune builds up at a faster and fiercer rate than the can</span>
  <span id="freeText13374213794462160581" style="display:none"><i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i> is about friendship but in some strange way it reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/333538.The_Castle" title="The Castle by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Castle</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5223.Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">Franz Kafka</a>…<br />Between a man and freedom there is always a wall of bureaucracy…<br />“Paul’s mind quite simply exploded because he could not discard his intellectual fortune fast enough. In the same way Nietzsche’s mind exploded, just as all the other mad philosophical minds exploded, because they could no longer sustain the pace. Their intellectual fortune builds up at a faster and fiercer rate than the can discard it, then one day the mind explodes and they are dead.”<br />“For in much wisdom is much grief: and he that increaseth knowledge increaseth sorrow.”<br />Ecclesiastes 1:18.</span>

<div id="review_1767406323" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1767406323" />
    <a title="Nora Barnacle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9162933-nora-barnacle"><img alt="Nora Barnacle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585003524p2/9162933.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1767406323?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nora Barnacle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nora Barnacle" href="/user/show/9162933-nora-barnacle">Nora Barnacle</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788679581006" href="/book/show/29852784-vitgen-tajnov-ne-ak">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1767406323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17808689124544327827">Svašta bih sad mogla da napišem: od himne, do anateme. Neka ovo bude prvi utisak, bez ocene.<br/>Paul Vitgenštajn, kao i epizode iz Bernhardovog života su osnove na kojima stoji ovaj maestralni prikaz sasvim perverznog straha od opšteg bezumlja koje pametnog čoveka mora da razboli: od tuberkuloze ili šizofrenije, svejedno.<br/>Ovaj roman je, pre svega, esencija prestravljenosti i gneva koji vode u ludilo, vapaj davljenika koji se okreće u vrtlogu nesavladive zavisti – hroničnog bolesnika – prema svemu što</span>
  <span id="freeText17808689124544327827" style="display:none">Svašta bih sad mogla da napišem: od himne, do anateme. Neka ovo bude prvi utisak, bez ocene.<br />Paul Vitgenštajn, kao i epizode iz Bernhardovog života su osnove na kojima stoji ovaj maestralni prikaz sasvim perverznog straha od opšteg bezumlja koje pametnog čoveka mora da razboli: od tuberkuloze ili šizofrenije, svejedno.<br />Ovaj roman je, pre svega, esencija prestravljenosti i gneva koji vode u ludilo, vapaj davljenika koji se okreće u vrtlogu nesavladive zavisti – hroničnog bolesnika – prema svemu što nije bolesno. Bernhard je zgrožen, razočaran, izdan, očajan i besan, besan, besan. Besan na glupost, sunovrat duha, bolest i smrt, a, opet, ludo zaljubljen u život, nije se naslušao muzike, nagledao slika, načitao novina, narazgovarao. I sve je odvratno, na čelu sa njim samim koji, iako sve vidi i zna, ne može da se suprotstavi. Ni sebi, ni tom svom strahu.<br />Demosnko ludilo koje je ovde naštelovano u centralnu figuru (iako ja mislim da nije) je Bernhardov lajt motiv i provejava i na drugim meni poznatim mestima (čitala sam još „Poremećaj“ i „Gubitnik“ ), ali u blažem obliku. <br />Najjači utisak mi je, ipak, forma. Moguće zato što nju mogu da artikulišem: monolitnošću i nizanjem stilskih figura (pre svega nebrojenim anaforama, epiforama i simplohama) potpuno me je ubedio da je izuzetnno muzikalan majstor stila, koji, kad poželi, može da zaliči na šizofrenog silovatelja hartije: knjiga zvuči kao da je neko sa ozbiljnom psihijatrijskom dijagnozom seo, otvorio usta i u dahu ispričao sadržaj ovih stotinak stranica, dok slušalac preko puta – čitalac – sve vreme gleda kako se ovaj znoji, gubi nit pa se vraća, menja mišljenje, krivi sebe, pa prašta, onda krivi sve druge, pa ih razume i opravdava, pa ne zna ko je kriv i da li uopšte ima krivih... a u pozadini kuca nekakav metronom, ili kaplje slavina – još bolje! Prevodilac Sanja Karanović je dobro uradila posao, ali verujem da je na nemačkom utisak daleko bolji, pa, ko može – neka ne propusti.<br /><br />Za formu, 5 ko vrata i dubok naklon.<br />Ipak, ovo je mučna, grozomorna kavez - knjiga, i ali vrhunska književnost<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17808689124544327827" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1715190265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6464174541075631940">At first I expected an autobiographical tale of friendship between author and Paul Wittgenstein, nephew of Ludwig Wittgenstein. But then it is so much more, there were discussions about Austrian society. My favourite is the literary prize episode, I found it hilarious.<br/><br/>But at the end, the author was discussing his friend, Paul, at the last stages of his life. It was so sad (At that late part I was torn between 4 or 5 star rating, I was so moved by Paul's situation). <br/><br/>The author combined the absurd</span>
  <span id="freeText6464174541075631940" style="display:none">At first I expected an autobiographical tale of friendship between author and Paul Wittgenstein, nephew of Ludwig Wittgenstein. But then it is so much more, there were discussions about Austrian society. My favourite is the literary prize episode, I found it hilarious.<br /><br />But at the end, the author was discussing his friend, Paul, at the last stages of his life. It was so sad (At that late part I was torn between 4 or 5 star rating, I was so moved by Paul's situation). <br /><br />The author combined the absurdity of Austrian Theatre society with Paul's friendship beautifully at the end of book, then followed by the final downfall of Paul. That end part of book deserves 5 star rating.<br /><br />I have problem with writing style, maybe I am not ready yet with this continuous writing with non-linear timeline. Fortunately the end was worth the effort.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3567045026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4787176597412790455">يستدعي الكاتب "توماس برنهارد" صديقه الأوحد "باول" من الذاكرة كشظايا مرايا مُحطمة...هل كان حقاً يُشكل صورتها الأولى ليتمكن من رؤيته على ما كان عليه...أم كان يقف في مواجهة صادقة مع ذاته ليكشف عن إذعانه للخوف من الموت بينما كان صديقه يحتضر وحيداً حبيساً بشرنقة اليأس...؟؟<br/>لقد جمعتهما صداقة فريدة...استثنائية ، تشاركا سوياً الإصغاء...التلقي..والاهتمام... ، حتى هشاشة الوجود الانساني أمام المرض والموت....<br/>وبالرغم من كونهما متشابهين ولكنهما كانا مختلفين إلى أقصى حد...<br/>فإن كان "توماس" قد غذى وجوده بجنونه...ف</span>
  <span id="freeText4787176597412790455" style="display:none">يستدعي الكاتب "توماس برنهارد" صديقه الأوحد "باول" من الذاكرة كشظايا مرايا مُحطمة...هل كان حقاً يُشكل صورتها الأولى ليتمكن من رؤيته على ما كان عليه...أم كان يقف في مواجهة صادقة مع ذاته ليكشف عن إذعانه للخوف من الموت بينما كان صديقه يحتضر وحيداً حبيساً بشرنقة اليأس...؟؟<br />لقد جمعتهما صداقة فريدة...استثنائية ، تشاركا سوياً الإصغاء...التلقي..والاهتمام... ، حتى هشاشة الوجود الانساني أمام المرض والموت....<br />وبالرغم من كونهما متشابهين ولكنهما كانا مختلفين إلى أقصى حد...<br />فإن كان "توماس" قد غذى وجوده بجنونه...ف"باول" التهم جنونه وجوده....<br />معادلة صعبة..مُرهقة.. ، تكشف عن صداقة تطلبت نضالاً شاقاً ومُضنياً للحفاظ عليها من براثن الجنون....<br />تراءى لي كما لو أن حواسهما لم تكن تتوقف عن تلقي رسائل من كل ما يدور حولهما...ويسارعان بالنفاذ إلى جوهرها ، يغرقونها بتأملاتهما الفلسفية وثراءهما الفكري وذائقتهما الموسيقية الأوبرالية...<br />ومع ذلك يكيلا الاتهامات اللعينة لعالم قبيح..بليد ، فلم يكن لشيء أن ينجو من قبضتهما الساخرة التهكمية...<br />في زخم هذه الحياة أسرتني ضحكة "باول" ...تناهى إلى مسامعي رنينها وهو يرتطم بجدران قاعات الأوبرا...وقد انفطر قلبي عندما ذوت تلك الضحكة وتوارت داخل قفص مستشفى الأمراض العقلية...<br />كان على حد قول الكاتب "بلسماً وجعل وجوده افضل على أي حال..."<br />باول قال يوماً لصديقه :<br /><b> مئتا صديق سيحضرون دفني ولابد من أن تُلقي أنت على قبري كلمة تأبيني....<br />ولكن "توماس" لم يكن حاضراً آنذاك...بل لم يستطع أن يزور قبره حتى يوم كتابة هذا العمل الإنساني الذي يعبق دفئاً.. رهافة..وهشاشة...</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="4787176597412790455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3028065134" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3028065134" />
    <a title="Kaggelo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28530103-kaggelo"><img alt="Kaggelo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478853859p2/28530103.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3028065134?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaggelo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaggelo" href="/user/show/28530103-kaggelo">Kaggelo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789600501131" href="/book/show/17202388">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3028065134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3791036960046177373">Ο γνωστός Bernhard. Καταιγιστικός, εξομολογητικός, ειλικρινής, συγκινητικός. Είναι η εξιστόρηση μιας πολύ βαθιάς και ολοκληρωτικής φιλίας ανάμεσα στον συγγραφέα και στον ανιψιό του γνωστού φιλοσόφου. Μου έκανε ιδιαίτερη εντύπωση το τέλος. Από τα καλύτερα που έχω διαβάσει. Πολύ πικρό, αναπάντεχα ειλικρινές. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1279779725" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1279779725" />
    <a title="Deepthi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7298095-deepthi"><img alt="Deepthi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436848146p2/7298095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1279779725?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deepthi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deepthi" href="/user/show/7298095-deepthi">Deepthi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400077564" href="/book/show/6327556-wittgenstein-s-nephew">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298095-deepthi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1279779725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7999792435068224379">Bernhard likes very few things, especially people. To paraphrase his narrator, who at a certain instance in the novel ruthlessly points out, one doesn't even have to use all the five fingers of one hand to count the things, ideas or ideals of the society he is in good terms with. A sample of his raw judgement: <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>For let us not deceive ourselves: most of the minds we associate with are housed in heads that have little more to offer than overgrown potatoes, stuck on top of whining and tastelessly</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText7999792435068224379" style="display:none">Bernhard likes very few things, especially people. To paraphrase his narrator, who at a certain instance in the novel ruthlessly points out, one doesn't even have to use all the five fingers of one hand to count the things, ideas or ideals of the society he is in good terms with. A sample of his raw judgement: <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>For let us not deceive ourselves: most of the minds we associate with are housed in heads that have little more to offer than overgrown potatoes, stuck on top of whining and tastelessly clad bodies and eking out a pathetic existence that does not even merit our pity. </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Bernhard pleases me, even though I cringe at his cynicism once in a while. Maybe not cringe, but I do think his narrators are "too unhappy" or "too unwilling" to bend to any norms that are not their own. All I want to do is give them a giant hug and tell them that the world isn't such a bad place after all and people aren't that intolerable. I know Bernhard would shun me away by calling me ignorant and naive. Well, he is right too. Even though we tell ourselves all the lies I have mentioned above, and try to "fit" into the socially constructed norms, which we subconsciously know aren't our cup of tea and that we are creating this palace of delusion just to avoid friction and life-long loneliness, we always, yes always, want to do otherwise. For we are the beings who think and like to brood over things. For we want to sit with our friend(s) in silence while listening to music or staring at paintings or dancing to the waves of the ocean and say nothing. For we want to discuss about ideas and not people. For we, believe it or not, are capable of more than the filthy mediocrity we thrive in and surround us with. That is where Bernhard shines. He says the way it is, and shows you the same as well. If you aren't good enough for him, he doesn't want you. He rips of all the layers of superficiality that our society is clad in and shames it by criticizing its ugly nakedness which is so unendurable that we choose to, weaklings that we are, wrap it back again in its shiny superfluous garments and take a sigh of relief.<br /><br /><i>Wittgenstein's Nephew</i>, as it says on the cover, is part fiction and part memoir. In 1967, Bernhard and Paul Wittgenstein, nephew of the enigmatic (or so I find him) philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein, were admitted in two separate wings of a Viennese hospital. The former was in for a certain lung disease and the other for his sudden bursts of mental illness. Throughout the novel, Bernhard's narrator speaks in one paragraph with no dialogues (as the usual Bernhard goes) explaining his relationship with Paul, and how their friendship came to be and blossomed into a precious bond of a lifetime. Most of the reasons the narrator points out are their shared interests in music and philosophy. One cannot help but notice the obsessive comparisons the narrator draws, which is done quite occasionally throughout the novel, to point out their similarities. This only shows how much the narrator respects and values Paul's friendship. They both are sick with the society they live in, both are mad in their own way and both of them know how utterly hopeless their lives are. The narrator discusses then-Austrian society (or has anything changed after all), its shallowness, with Paul as his reason to do so. He talks about his unique relationship with Paul prior to their illness, during their recovery and after leaving the <i>grotesque</i> hospital and its inmates. Then, the novel slowly progresses to Paul's death and his very obvious sad end, which becomes unbearable to the narrator, so much so that he avoids any slight chance of a meeting with him. His fear of the sick and dying is understandable. If you read this book, you will know why.<br /><br />Even though the narrator is full of distaste and scorn, I would like to believe that I am a little like him, who wants to rot in his own solitude even if this society has to offer all its glittery nonsense and false glee. While this society celebrates my doom with its fake "oh-s" and "ah-s", I want to die next to that one friend whom I can count on that one finger of that one hand of Bernhard's narrator with a smile on my face and a feeling in my heart that I was understood. I cannot help but wonder if this sort of sick society is the reason why people like Ludwig and Bernhard are deemed mad, and why people like Paul turn mad.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7999792435068224379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
